👋 Welcome & Expectations — 29 min

👋 Welcome and Expectations — 29 min

Antonio Perez

⏳ Estimated time: 4 min

Welcome! 🤖

We are thrilled to have you on board! To start setting expectations, here is what you need to do before the welcome session next Wednesday, May 27 at 5:30 PM CEST!

  • 💬 Read the Expectations message.

  • 🎥 Watch the Program Overview video.

  • 👀 Take a look at the Useful Links.

  • 🚦 Learn the emoji system.

Are you ready? 💪

AI4Devs is a live online program that prepares you to harness the potential of code assistants and other AI-powered tools in your day-to-day work. It doesn't just teach you how to use these tools; you will also improve your knowledge of software engineering best practices, essential soft skills in this new context, and above all your mindset and attitude toward the changes coming to the industry.

AI4Devs 🤖 is a 20-week program divided into 2-hour group sessions (38 in total), a 30-hour individual project, and more than 10 hours of recorded content and additional material. It will require a minimum of 4 and an average of 6 hours of work per week.

🗓 The program:

Session 1 - The 3 pillars of effective use of AI copilots in development

Session 2 - Project setup and Spec Driven Development

Session 3 - Hands-on implementation with AI copilots

Session 4 - Effective project planning with AI-assisted development

Session 5 - Effective project documentation with AI support

Session 6 - Privacy, Ethics, and Regulation when using AI

Session 7 - AI Testing 1. Testing and quality with AI

Session 8 - Databases with AI

Session 9 - Backend with AI

Session 10 - Frontend with AI

Session 11 - AI Testing 2. Automated testing and QA with AI

Session 12 - Lab 1: Build your own technical documentation chatbot with Langchain and Streamlit - Part 1

Session 13 - DevSecOps I, deployment and monitoring with AI

Session 14 - Lab 1: Build your own technical documentation chatbot with Langchain and Streamlit - Part 2

Session 15 - DevSecOps II, monitoring, observability, and security by design

Session 16 - Lab 2. Multi-agents

Session 17 - The future of the role I

Session 18 - The future of the role II

👋 Welcome & Expectations — 29 min

💬 Expectations — 9 min | AI4Devs 2026/06 Seniors

⏳ Estimated time: 6 min

Read this message carefully. It defines how the program works and what you can realistically expect from this experience.

⚠ Before we begin: something important

During the first few sessions, you may feel like you are not learning anything new.

This is normal.

The first weeks are designed to align the level of the group and establish a common working foundation. If you already have experience, this block will help you better structure the way you work and consolidate a methodology that you will apply throughout the entire program.

That feeling fades as you move forward.

It does not mean the program is not for you.

👉 What can you expect from us during the program?

  • Teaching by example. We want you to achieve excellence, and we will demonstrate that level in every action and decision.

  • Teaching you how to make decisions. We are not here to give you step-by-step instructions on what to do in every situation, since every project and company is a world of its own. Instead, you will be exposed to new situations and discover different approaches to tackle them (not only from the mentors, but also from your peers). We want you to develop the judgment to choose the right tool for each context, and when and how to use it.

  • 8x5 availability (and beyond — don't worry, we live here!). We are in the course WhatsApp group and also available privately. Whatever your question or difficulty, we are now your team. You can count on us for anything personal or related to AI4Devs, and on the Lidr Support Team for any technical issues.

  • Presenting you with real day-to-day situations from software teams. We will focus on common problems and dynamics in tech companies.

  • Punctuality in every live session. We will meet on Zoom at the scheduled time and will be connected 5 minutes early to start on time.

  • A variety of session formats. We will adapt each session to the situation: workshops, open debates, collaborative work in breakout rooms, Q&A rounds, or informal conversations.

  • Requesting continuous feedback. Constant improvement is key to excellence, and we adapt the program to your needs based on your comments.

  • Improving quickly based on your input. We will do everything possible to help you achieve your goals.

👉 What we expect from you:

  • Unlearn what you think a training program is. This is not a traditional course. It is a project in which you develop yourself, and the other participants are not just students, but your team. Your individual success depends on the success of the group. This is the key mindset.

  • Behave at all times in accordance with the AI4Devs General Terms and Conditions and with our community's Code of Conduct.

  • Prepare technically before each session. The main tools will be MightyNetworks, Zoom, and Drive, but we will also use GitHub, Miro, Airtable, and others. Make sure you have a good internet connection, good sound, good lighting, and a distraction-free environment. Keeping your camera on at all times is mandatory.

  • Arrive prepared for each live session. To make the most of our live time together, we will assume a minimum level of preparation. This means having consumed 100% of the mandatory content (videos, documentation) and having worked on the exercises. The more time you invest, the better the results.

  • Bring questions and doubts to the live sessions. With the flipped-classroom approach, you are the ones who set the pace. We will focus on the aspects you find most relevant.

  • Ask questions in public, not in private. We are not the only ones available to help you; your team, a group of great international talents, is also here to support you. In addition, you will surface questions that others may have, enriching everyone's experience.

  • Prepare proposals and solutions to exercises in whatever format you prefer. Document, presentation, video... although we especially recommend GitHub and Notion.

  • Demonstrate proactivity, initiative, curiosity, critical thinking, and a team attitude. We expect senior behavior in all your actions. Take it seriously: these are essential elements to make the most of this experience.

  • Give constructive and actionable feedback. The key to improvement is contributing solutions, not just pointing out problems. If something isn't working, tell us what and suggest a viable improvement.

  • Exercise review. To ensure a fair and agile process, we will select a random sample of 10 submitted exercises for review and correction. Based on this, we will make general observations that will help the whole group improve. If your exercise is not selected, don't worry: you can benefit from the observations and apply the lessons learned in your next submissions.

📍 This last point is fundamental. You will have different ways to share your feedback with us:

1⃣ At the end of each live session, a ROTI (Return On Time Invested) survey will be available on the platform, 15 minutes before the session ends.

2⃣ At the end of each module, you will have an optional survey to evaluate the content and the exercise of that module.

3⃣ In addition, in the "Useful Links" section, you have a form to leave more specific and detailed feedback, either anonymously or with your name, whichever you prefer.

Remember: we cannot promise to implement every suggestion, but we will evaluate 100% of them.

Finally, enjoy the process!

As you can see, both sides must make a conscious effort for AI4Devs to be a success.

We are 200% in,

How about you?

👋 Welcome & Expectations — 29 min

🎥 Program Overview 🤖 — 6 min | AI4Devs 2026/06 Seniors

⏳ Estimated time: 6 min

In this section you will understand how AI4Devs really works: not just what you are going to learn, but how you are going to learn it and with what mindset.

Our way of teaching is neither theoretical nor passive. It is designed for you to develop a real advantage in your day-to-day work as a developer.

You are not here to "consume content". You are here to train.

We work with principles such as learning by doing, learning by teaching, Shu-Ha-Ri, and continuous improvement, because these are what make the difference between someone who understands AI... and someone who uses it with impact.

The goal is not just for you to know how to use AI tools or apply engineering best practices.
The goal is for you to develop a way of working that is more efficient, more autonomous, and more results-oriented.

If you do the program well, you won't just improve your code.
It will change the way you approach any technical problem.

1. The Flipped Classroom learning model

At AI4Devs we use the flipped classroom model, because it is the one that works best when the goal is learning to build.

The approach is simple:

  • You learn the concepts on your own, through structured content (videos, resources, examples).

  • You use the live sessions to apply, not to listen to theory.

This allows us to make the most of our time together on what really adds value: building, making mistakes, iterating, and understanding.

In practice:

  • You arrive at the session with a minimal foundation already worked on.

  • During the session, you apply it to real cases.

  • The mentor doesn't "explain", but rather guides, corrects, and raises the bar.

This turns learning into something active, not passive.

Why do we work this way?

Because in the real world:

  • Nobody gives you the solution step by step.

  • Nobody explains everything before you start.

  • And the value lies in knowing how to solve, not in knowing how to repeat.

This model trains you for exactly that.

What it means for you

This approach requires a bit more commitment, but it also gives you much more in return:

  • Greater autonomy

  • Better problem-solving skills

  • More speed in learning new tools

  • Better technical judgment

2. Language used in the program

Throughout the program we will work primarily with JavaScript and TypeScript, both on the backend and the frontend.

This is no coincidence:

  • It is the most widespread ecosystem

  • It allows for fast iteration

  • It is flexible enough to work with AI and APIs

In addition, we will use GitHub as our base working environment, since it is part of any developer's day-to-day work.

If you are not familiar with the workflow (fork + pull request), here is a clear and practical guide to get started:

👉 https://lidr.notion.site/C-mo-hacer-tu-primer-Pull-Request-en-GitHub-341a4393278180fe84fae1c4db30048b?source=copy_link

In short, this process consists of making a copy (fork) of a repository, working on it, and submitting your changes via a pull request so they can be reviewed and merged

3. ROTI Feedback

At the end of each session, we will ask you to complete a ROTI (Return On Time Invested).

It is not a formality. It is a key tool for improving the program in real time.

It allows us to understand:

  • Whether the session added value

  • What worked

  • What we need to adjust

And it allows us to do so quickly, not when it's already too late.

In fact, a large part of the program's improvements come directly from the feedback of students like you

You can leave your feedback anonymously or publicly here: https://lidr.typeform.com/feedback-ai4dev

All the content we have developed is valuable for your training as an AI-augmented developer.

However, here is our emoji system so you can approach our content more effectively.

  • 🔴 Mandatory Content: Content you must consume before the live sessions to maximize your learning. We will assume we are all at the same level when the session starts.
  • 🟢 Optional Content: Content you can consume whenever you like to expand your knowledge without affecting your preparation for the live sessions.
  • 🎥 On-demand content: Previously recorded sessions that will help provide context and key concepts for the online classes and exercises.
  • ✍️ Mandatory tasks: Learning by doing! With these exercises you will bring the concepts we teach into YOUR reality so you start seeing real results and progress.
  • 💪 Optional tasks: The extra mile — these are exercises that will add invaluable value but will not affect your performance during the course.

👋 Welcome & Expectations — 29 min

📜 Repository Management Policy | AI4Devs 2026/06 Seniors

1. Objective
To establish clear guidelines for the management of project repositories within the IA4Devs program, balancing students' professional visibility with the protection of sensitive information.

2. Repository classification

2.1 Public repositories
Repositories may be public when they meet the following conditions:

  • The project is presentable as part of the student's professional portfolio

  • It does not contain sensitive or confidential information

  • It includes adequate documentation (README) explaining the purpose and workings of the project

2.2 Private repositories
Keeping repositories private is recommended in the following cases:

  • The project contains credentials, tokens, or access keys

  • Real APIs or services with sensitive data are used

  • There is internal information or information not intended for public distribution

  • The project is not ready to be shared externally

3. Mandatory best practices
For all repositories, especially public ones, the following practices must be followed:

  • Do not include .env files in the repository

  • Do not expose API keys, tokens, passwords, or credentials

  • Do not include real user data or personal information

  • Use .env.example files to document required variables

  • Use fictitious or test values in shared configurations

  • Include a README with clear installation and usage instructions

4. Responsibility
Each student is responsible for the content they publish in their repositories.
IA4Devs is not liable for the exposure of sensitive information resulting from improper use of these practices.

5. Visibility approach
Publishing repositories as public projects is part of students' professional development, facilitating portfolio building and exposure within the tech ecosystem.

It is recommended to consciously evaluate which projects to share publicly and under what conditions.

6. Additional recommendations

  • Review the repository before making it public

  • Use secret-scanning tools whenever possible

  • Keep the repository up to date with good documentation practices

  • When in doubt, opt to keep the repository private

Pre-course: Introduction to AI in Software Development — 102 min

🎥 The current role of AI in software development 🔴 — 12 min | AI4Devs 2026/06 Seniors

⏳ Estimated time: 12 min

The current role of AI in software development

In February 2026, Andrej Karpathy — one of the founders of OpenAI — declared that "vibe coding" was already out of fashion and proposed a different term for 2026: agentic engineering. The phrase is timely: in 18 months we went from "AI suggests lines to me" to "AI executes tasks for me for 200 minutes straight". This pre-course starts from there.

The paradigm shift in 4 years

The bottleneck is moving: it's no longer writing code, but defining what to build, providing effective context, and validating that what gets generated is useful.

What the hard data says (2025–2026)

Near-universal adoption

  • Stack Overflow Developer Survey 2025 (49,000 responses, 177 countries): 84% of developers use or plan to use AI tools (vs. 76% in 2024). 51% use them every day.

  • DORA 2025 (Google Cloud, ~5,000 professionals): 90% use AI at work (+14 points vs. 2024).

  • GitHub Octoverse 2025: 180 million devs on GitHub (+36M in one year, ≈1 new dev/second). 80% of new developers use Copilot in their first week.

A curious fact from Octoverse 2025

For the first time in more than a decade, TypeScript overtook Python and JavaScript as the most used language on GitHub (August 2025). GitHub attributes this to the "convenience loop" with AI: typed languages generate more useful guardrails for LLMs, which improves the code generation experience, which increases their usage, which feeds the cycle back.

About the programming language: The live demos and exercises in the program will be developed primarily in JavaScript and TypeScript. However, it is not a requirement to know them beforehand nor to adopt them mandatorily. The concepts of agentic engineering, AI tool usage, and workflows you will learn apply to any technology stack. You can complete the exercises and the final project in the language you already master.

But trust is falling

  • Stack Overflow 2025: trust in AI answers fell from 40% (2024) to 29% (2025). 46% actively distrust them.

  • Only 3% of developers report "high trust" in AI output.

  • 75% still prefer asking a human when they doubt the AI's answer.

The productivity-perception paradox

Here is the data point that should most challenge your assumptions before starting the master's program:

METR study (July 2025), RCT with 16 experienced developers on mature open source repositories (≥22k stars, ≥1M LOC), across 246 real tasks with Cursor Pro + Claude 3.5/3.7 Sonnet:

  • The devs predicted they would be 24% faster with AI.

  • After the experiment, they believed they had been 20% faster.

  • In reality they were 19% slower.

🔗 Full study: METR — Measuring the Impact of Early-2025 AI on Experienced Open-Source Developer Productivity

📺 Andrej Karpathy discusses this study and other data in his keynote "Software 3.0" (Y Combinator AI Startup School, June 2025):

How to read this study without drawing the wrong conclusion

The "19% slower" is a solid data point within its sample and specific moment (early 2025, huge and mature codebases), not a universal law. METR itself published (February 2026) that it is redesigning the experiment due to selection biases. What matters for you is:

  • In greenfield + simple tasks, the gains are real and sometimes massive (Stanford reports up to +30–40% in these contexts).

  • In legacy, complex codebases with implicit conventions, AI introduces validation overhead, rework, and context-switching. And perception deceives.

  • Measure > assume. That's the first operating principle of the master's program.

The other uncomfortable finding (Stanford, October 2025)

A paper on arXiv (2510.10165) analyzed what happened after the introduction of Copilot in teams:

  • Core devs (more experienced) review +6.5% more code.

  • But their production of original code falls −19%.

The technical debt generated by AI shifts toward the seniors, who spend more time on code review.

Software 3.0 and agentic engineering (Karpathy)

In his talk on June 17, 2025 at the AI Startup School, Andrej Karpathy formalized:

  • Software 1.0: classic code written by humans.

  • Software 2.0: neural networks (the weights are the "code").

  • Software 3.0: LLMs programmable in natural language, with prompts as code and the context window as memory.

📖 Transcript and analysis: Latent Space — Andrej Karpathy on Software 3.0

Vibe coding was coined by Karpathy on X on February 2, 2025: going with the flow and writing software by conversing with the LLM, "forgetting that the code exists".

🚨 February 2026 update: Karpathy himself declared that "vibe coding is already out of fashion" and proposed agentic engineering to distinguish professional use with supervision, oversight, and rigor.

"Agentic because by default you're no longer writing 99% of the code, you're orchestrating agents and supervising. Engineering because there's an art and a science that can be learned."

That difference — between vibe coding (mood, demos) and agentic engineering (supervision, production) — is exactly the line this master's program crosses.

What's happening with the money

To understand the urgency of the moment, the investment numbers speak for themselves (figures as of April 2026):

  • Cursor (Anysphere): USD 2,000M ARR in February 2026 — the fastest B2B company to reach USD 2B. Discussed valuation: USD 50–60B.

  • Cognition (Devin + Windsurf): round under discussion at a USD 25,000M valuation (vs. USD 10.2B in September 2025).

  • Total AI coding tools market: estimated between USD 7.4B and USD 12.8B in 2026, with projections of USD 26–30B toward 2030.

  • GitHub Copilot: 20 million total users (July 2025), 4.7 million paying (January 2026, +75% YoY), 90% of the Fortune 100 have adopted it.

💡 Don't memorize these figures. What matters is the magnitude: at no point in the history of software development has there been this level of investment and concentration in tools that change how the individual dev works. That makes it mandatory to have an informed opinion on how to use them.

The elephant in the room: the employment data

  • SignalFire State of Talent 2025: Big Tech cut hiring of recent graduates by −25% in 2024 vs. 2023. Grads now represent only 7% of Big Tech hiring (vs. ~15% pre-pandemic).

  • Stanford Digital Economy Lab 2025: employment of software developers aged 22–25 fell ~20% since late 2022. That of the 35–49 age group rose +9% in the same period.

  • The same companies raised hiring of devs with 2–5 years of experience by +27%.

📺 Erik Brynjolfsson (Stanford) explains the democratization of Seniority: The "Canary in the Coal Mine" effect

The "senior dev" role is not being replaced, it is being redefined. And this master's program is one of the ways to make that transition on purpose instead of suffering it.

The actionable takeaways from this page

  1. Don't expect automatic productivity gains. Expecting 10–20% on average is realistic; greenfield can deliver much more, legacy can be negative. Measure your specific case.

  2. AI shifts load toward you as a senior. More code review, more validation, more architectural decisions. Adjust your definition of Done.

  3. The bottleneck moves to problem framing and context engineering. These will be the pillars of the first sessions of the master's program.

  4. The market is concentrating fast. You don't need to master 10 tools, you need to master 1–2 well and understand how they connect.

Resources to go deeper

  • 📺 Andrej Karpathy — Software 3.0 (YC AI Startup School, June 2025) — 40 min, in English with subtitles.

  • 📖 METR — Measuring the Impact of Early-2025 AI on Experienced OS Devs — the 19% study.

  • 📖 Stack Overflow Developer Survey 2025 — AI — the full adoption and trust data.

  • 📖 GitHub Octoverse 2025 — the state of the dev ecosystem.

  • 📖 DORA 2025: State of AI-assisted Software Development — what AI amplifies in real teams.

  • 📺 dotCSV — Vibe coding: the future of programming? (channel in Spanish, search for "vibe coding")

👉 When you finish reading, leave in the comments: what percentage of your code in the last month was written or suggested by AI, and where has it made you slower than you expected? It's the best way to start the course with honesty.

Pre-course: Introduction to AI in Software Development — 102 min

🎥 The future of the developer: augmented by AI 🔴 — 13 min | AI4Devs 2026/06 Seniors

⏳ Estimated time: 13 min

The future of the developer: augmented by AI

If the previous page left you with the feeling that something is changing fast, this page answers the uncomfortable question: what does that mean for your specific career as a senior? The short answer: your role doesn't disappear, it gets redefined. But the redefinition is neither optional nor gradual.

The fundamental shift: from programmer to orchestrator

In 2022 we talked about "copilots" that suggested code line by line. In 2026, tools like Cursor 2.0 run up to 8 agents in parallel, Claude Code executes tasks for hours in GitHub Actions, and Devin works asynchronously on tickets assigned from Jira.

The developer's new role has two faces depending on experience level. And the data shows the asymmetry is brutal.

For senior / mid developers

  • Architect and supervisor: you define the structure, review the generated code, ensure it meets standards.

  • Edge case detector: AI "works for the happy path" — your job is to think about everything that can go wrong.

  • Quality guardian: 66% of developers report that AI solutions are "almost right, but not quite" (Stack Overflow Developer Survey 2025).

For junior developers

  • Impact multiplier: studies show greater productivity gains among less experienced developers, but...

  • Learning accelerator or trap: seeing solved patterns without understanding them can produce engineers who depend on the tool without building their own intuition.

The evolved skills triad

The classic triangle (soft skills + systems vision + tool usage) remains valid, but with important nuances for 2026.

1. Soft skills + problem framing

Oguz Acar (King's College London, HBR 2023) formulated it before anyone else: with LLMs getting ever better at natural language, what's scarce is no longer writing prompts — it's framing the problem well.

"Without a well-formulated problem, not even the best prompt helps."

A survey Acar cites: 85% of the C-suite state that their organizations "don't diagnose problems well". That skill — decomposing an ambiguous problem into solvable sub-problems — is the one most valued in seniors from 2026 onward.

Concrete skills that become critical:

  • Communication with agents: writing effective context (which files matter, which constraints apply, which patterns to follow).

  • Critical code review: detecting over-engineering, solutions that "work" but aren't maintainable, security risks.

  • Human collaboration: paradoxically, the more code the AI writes, the more human coordination matters (mentoring, architectural decisions, team alignment).

2. Strategic vision + systems thinking

DORA 2025 (Google Cloud, ~5,000 professionals) introduces the AI Capabilities Model: 7 organizational capabilities (internal platform quality, clarity of workflows, data, culture of experimentation, user focus, etc.) that multiply AI's positive impact.

The report's central quote:

"AI doesn't fix a team; it amplifies what's already there." — DORA 2025: State of AI-assisted Software Development

In other words: AI turns good teams into excellent ones, and mediocre teams into chaotic ones. The multiplier cuts both ways.

Skills a senior applies:

  • Problem decomposition: breaking a complex problem into tasks an agent can execute autonomously.

  • Judgment on when to use (and NOT use) AI: trade-offs of cost, privacy, compliance, maintainability.

  • High-level architecture: agents implement, but design decisions remain human.

3. Agent orchestration (the new skill)

This is the skill that didn't exist 18 months ago and now distinguishes the seniors who are mastering the transition:

  • From assistant to agent: it's not about autocomplete, but about delegating complete tasks — "implement OAuth authentication with Google", "refactor this module to the Repository pattern".

  • Output verification: the agent generates, you verify that it works, is secure, maintainable, and meets the non-obvious requirements.

  • Effective iteration: giving clear feedback, adjusting context, regenerating — without falling into the infinite loop of "almost right, but not quite".

📺 Andrej Karpathy explains this transition in his talk "Software Is Changing (Again)" at YC AI Startup School (June 2025), where he introduces the concept of the "autonomy slider" and how the dev role evolves:

A sober reading: what this data does NOT say

The data reflects both the impact of AI and macro factors (interest rate hikes 2022–2023, post-pandemic Big Tech layoffs). It's hard to separate causes. But the pattern is clear-cut:

  • AI disproportionately affects junior roles.

  • Senior roles remain stable or grow — the experience to supervise and architect becomes more valuable.

  • Hiring concentrates at 2–5 years of experience: the "sweet spot" where the dev can operate agents with autonomy but already has judgment.

"Vibe coding": reality vs. marketing

Much has been said about vibe coding — describing what you want in natural language and having AI implement it. Replit, Cursor, Lovable, and others have turned it into technical reality.

But professional adoption is much slower than the marketing suggests. Stack Overflow 2025:

  • 52% of developers don't use agents or use only simple AI.

  • 38% don't plan to adopt them.

  • 75% prefer asking a human when they doubt the AI's answer.

And in September 2025, Fast Company reported the "vibe coding hangover": senior engineers describing "development hell" when they receive vibe-coded code to maintain.

💡 Important: Andrej Karpathy himself declared in February 2026 that "vibe coding is already passé" and proposed agentic engineering as the term for professional use with supervision, oversight, and rigor. This is exactly the line this master's program crosses: we don't teach vibe-coding, we teach agentic engineering.

The two frustrations senior devs report most with AI

Stack Overflow Developer Survey 2025 (49,000 responses):

The conclusion: AI agents are extraordinary for well-defined tasks and known patterns. They are limited for ambiguous problems, complex architecture, or contexts where "almost correct" isn't enough. Your job as a senior is to know exactly where that line is.

How the role changes concretely

Relevant industry voices (2025–2026)

  • Erik Brynjolfsson (Stanford): "Young people in exposed occupations are 'canaries in the coal mine'. If we use AI to augment and not just automate, we gain shared prosperity."

  • Heather Doshay (SignalFire): "AI won't take your job if you're the one who's best at using it."

  • Satya Nadella (Microsoft): 20–30% of Microsoft's internal code is already generated or suggested by AI. He insists junior devs must master the fundamentals of "computational thinking".

  • Dario Amodei (Anthropic): half of junior white-collar jobs could be affected by AI within 5 years. He reports that ~90% of Anthropic's code is AI-assisted.

  • Andrej Karpathy (February 2026): "Agentic engineering: agentic because by default you're no longer writing 99% of the code, you're orchestrating agents and supervising. Engineering because there's an art and a science that can be learned."

The actionable takeaways from this page

  1. The senior role doesn't disappear, it becomes critical. The multiplier "AI amplifies what's there" means your judgment, criteria, and architecture are worth more, not less.

  2. You invest time in problem framing, not prompt engineering. The scarce skill is framing the problem well before you start typing.

  3. Code review goes from occasional task to core skill. When 1 in 5 reviews is already done by an AI, your role as human reviewer becomes "critical editor of the automatic editor".

  4. If you manage a team, the pyramid changes. Fewer juniors, more mid-level, and the need to mentor skills that used to be learned on their own (debugging from scratch, reading someone else's codebase).

  5. Keep the craft. The scariest data is about juniors who depend so much on AI that they don't build intuition. As a senior, your advantage is having programmed without AI — don't lose it.

🎥 The assistant ecosystem in 2026 🔴 — 23 min | AI4Devs 2026/06 Seniors

The assistant ecosystem in 2026

We constantly see new AI tools emerge, each optimized for different use cases. The big question is no longer "which one should you choose?", but "what level of autonomy do I need for this task?". This page gives you the map to answer that, plus the technical context that is turning isolated tools into an interconnected ecosystem.

The consolidation trend (last 12 months)

The market has undergone violent consolidation. The Windsurf saga sums it up better than any executive summary:

Other relevant moves:

  • Anysphere/Cursor acquired Supermaven (autocomplete) in November 2024 and Graphite (code review) in December 2025.

  • GitHub gets absorbed into Microsoft's CoreAI group after Thomas Dohmke's departure (August 2025) — a signal of strategic shift toward agents.

What does this mean for you? Big companies are absorbing or directly competing with innovative startups. Developers are increasingly looking for fewer fragmented tools and more integrated platforms that cover the entire workflow. Consolidation is not theoretical: it already happened.

Quick comparison (April 2026)

💡 Don't memorize these figures. They change week to week. What matters is the magnitude: no moment in dev tooling history has seen this level of investment and growth. This validates the paradigm shift — it's not hype.

Capabilities that are converging

All modern tools are moving toward a common set of capabilities:

1. Native multimodality

Working with text alone is no longer enough. GPT-5.x, Claude 4.6/4.7, and Gemini 3 process:

  • Text (code, documentation, prompts).

  • Images (mockups, diagrams, UI-to-code screenshots).

  • Audio (voice commands, transcriptions).

  • Video / streaming (share your screen and ask in real time).

Key trend of the last 6 months: leading tools are training their own models to reduce dependence on Anthropic/OpenAI:

  • Cursor Composer (October 2025) and Composer 2 (~March 2026, ~86% cheaper).

  • Cognition trains its own models for Devin in addition to using frontier ones.

  • GitHub experiments with models optimized for Copilot.

MCP: the standard that connects everything

So far we've seen that the tools are converging. Now comes the structural part: how they connect to each other. And the answer has a name:

What is MCP?

Model Context Protocol (MCP) is an open protocol that allows AI tools to connect with any data source or service through a universal standard. Anthropic announced it on November 25, 2024.

The standard analogy: MCP is to AI what USB was to devices. Instead of custom integrations for every tool↔service combination, there is a single protocol.

Before MCP, if you wanted your AI assistant to access Notion, you needed a specific integration for each tool (one for Claude, another for Cursor, another for ChatGPT...).

With MCP, Notion exposes an MCP server and any compatible tool can connect immediately.

Massive adoption in 18 months

Volumes (April 2026):

  • 97 million monthly downloads of MCP SDKs (vs. ~100 thousand at launch).

  • >10,000 active public MCP servers (official registry: ~2,000; PulseMCP indexes 5,500+).

Popular MCP servers today

  • Productivity: Notion, Slack, Google Drive, Gmail, Calendar, Linear, Jira.

  • Development: GitHub, Sentry, Postgres, MySQL, Snowflake, Filesystem, Memory.

  • APIs: Stripe, AWS S3, Brave Search, Puppeteer/Playwright.

Configuration example

In Claude Desktop / Cursor / Claude Code, the mcp.json file:

{
  "mcpServers": {
    "github": {
      "command": "npx",
      "args": ["-y", "@modelcontextprotocol/server-github"],
      "env": { "GITHUB_PERSONAL_ACCESS_TOKEN": "ghp_..." }
    },
    "postgres": {
      "command": "npx",
      "args": [
        "-y",
        "@modelcontextprotocol/server-postgres",
        "postgresql://user:pass@host/db"
      ]
    }
  }
}

File path depending on the client:

  • Claude Code: ~/.claude/mcp.json or per-project configuration.

  • Cursor: Settings → MCP.

  • Claude Desktop: Settings → Developer → Edit Config.

Real use cases (verified data)

  • Block (creators of Cash App) report that their employees save 50–75% of the time on common tasks with their agent goose connected via MCP to Snowflake, GitHub, Jira, Slack, and Drive.

  • Microsoft Sales Development Agent processed 61,734 leads (Jan–Nov 2025) and improved lead-to-opportunity conversion by +15.1% thanks to MCP↔Dynamics 365.

Try this now (5 min)

  1. If you use Claude Desktop: go to Settings → Extensions, search for "Notion", install with one click, and authenticate with OAuth.

  2. Ask Claude: "Create a page in my workspace with my notes from today".

  3. Watch how it reads and writes in your Notion without you touching anything else.

📖 Official MCP documentation: modelcontextprotocol.io

📺 MCP demo in Claude Code (channel in Spanish)

💡 Why it matters for the master's program: MCP is the "invisible infrastructure" on which almost all the demos in the upcoming sessions are built. Being clear on what it is now saves a mentor from having to explain it 5 times.

Which tool to choose in 2026?

The right question isn't "Copilot or Cursor or Claude". It's: what level of autonomy do I need for this task?

For fast autocomplete in the editor

  • GitHub Copilot (integrated in VS Code, already corporate-paid at many companies).

  • Cursor basic mode.

For multi-file tasks with supervision

  • Cursor 2.0 + Composer 2 (~USD 20/month) — if you prefer a visual IDE with a multi-agent UI.

  • Claude Code (included in Claude Pro USD 20/month) — if you prefer the terminal and CLAUDE.md.

  • Windsurf (~USD 15/month) — agentic IDE with Cascade.

For complex autonomous projects

  • Devin (USD 20/month + pay-per-use) — the most mature, best for enterprise. Goldman Sachs, Citi, Dell, Cisco, Palantir, Nubank, Mercado Libre use it in production.

  • Replit Agent — ideal for rapid prototypes in the browser.

For integrations with your work tools

Any tool with good MCP support — verify that it can connect to your services (Notion, Slack, GitHub, DB).

Key trend: tools with better MCP support gain a competitive advantage, because they access the developer's complete ecosystem. This is what we are teaching in this master's program as the primary tool: Claude Code, for its native MCP integration, its CLAUDE.md model for persistent context, and its official GitHub Actions support. Not because it's the only good one, but because it best embodies the "agentic engineering" paradigm that is the guiding thread of the program.

The consolidation moment: the smartphone analogy

We are witnessing the equivalent of the "smartphone" moment in development tools:

Before (2020–2023): Multiple specialized devices (GPS, camera, mp3, phone...) = multiple fragmented tools (linter, formatter, autocomplete, docs, testing...).

Now (2024–2026): One platform that does everything (smartphone) = one agentic platform with capabilities extensible via MCP (IDE + agent + connectors).

The winning bets (market data)

  1. Platforms with the best MCP ecosystem (more connectors = more useful).

  2. Companies that control both the base model AND the tool (Anthropic with Claude Code, Google with Gemini CLI, Cognition with Devin).

  3. IDEs that evolved into agents (Cursor, Windsurf) rather than startups that started as pure agents.

The actionable takeaways from this page

  1. Don't half-learn 5 tools. Master 1–2 deeply. The real learning curve is in how to give it context, not in how to open the menu.

  2. Prioritize tools with open standards (MCP) over closed ecosystems. What you connect today with MCP you will keep using even if you switch your primary tool.

  3. Your competitive advantage lies in how you configure your context (CLAUDE.md, prompts, skills, MCP connectors). That's what the master's program teaches.

  4. The market has consolidated. Don't wait for "next year's perfect tool". The ones that exist today are the ones you'll be using for a long time — we've already crossed the smartphone moment.

  5. MCP is not a feature, it's infrastructure. Learn what it is and how to configure it before session 1, because we'll be using it from S2 onward.

🎥 AI's Impact on the SDLC 🔴 — 18 min | AI4Devs 2026/06 Seniors

⏳ Estimated time: 18 min

So far you've seen data about the market, the developer role, and the tool ecosystem. Now comes the map: how AI changes each phase of the Software Development Life Cycle (SDLC), which tools operate in each one, and where each topic is covered across the master's 16 sessions. This lesson is your mental map of the program.

The SDLC phases (and why it matters to see them all)

When we think about "AI for developers," the reflex is to think of code generation. But that's only one of the points where AI changes the craft. A senior's day-to-day covers much more:

  • Requirements analysis

  • System design and data model

  • High-level documentation and specifications (user stories, tasks)

  • Implementation: infra, backend, frontend, DB; bug fixing, refactoring, security

  • Test generation: unit, E2E, integration

  • Change documentation and code reviews

  • Deployments

  • Monitoring and observability

Each of these phases has changed in the last 18 months. Let's go through the ones with the greatest impact.

How each SDLC phase changes with AI

💡 Reading the map: each cell in the "Master session" column is where we dive deep into that phase. If one session sparks more curiosity than another, that's a good sign — it means you've already identified your priority lever.

The L1–L5 framework: autonomy levels

To understand which tool to use in each phase, the industry adopted a 5-level autonomy framework (popularized in 2024–2025; used by Swarmia, Vellum, AWS, and others). It's not an IEEE standard, but it is the most widely used pragmatic taxonomy.

🚨 Important note: different vendors use slightly different variants (Vellum uses L0–L5, AWS talks about 4 levels). Take it as a widely used practical framework, not a universal law.

L1 — Code autocompletion

Inline token/line suggestions in the editor. The human accepts or rejects each suggestion.

Representative tools (April 2026):

  • GitHub Copilot completions — the pioneer, still the leader in enterprise adoption.

  • Supermaven — acquired by Cursor in November 2024; the standalone product was retired in late 2025.

  • Tabby — open source alternative for self-hosting.

  • Codeium / Windsurf inline — the Codeium legacy now inside the Windsurf ecosystem.

L2 — Task-level generation

The human describes a task; the AI produces a coherent block. The human verifies.

Representative tools:

  • ChatGPT, Claude.ai, Gemini — chat with code.

  • aider — open source CLI tool for code editing via LLM.

  • Cursor Chat inline, GitHub Copilot Chat — chat inside the editor.

L3 — Coding agents (in context)

The agent executes multiple steps (read/edit/test) under supervision, with a full paper trail in Git.

Main tools:

  • Cursor Composer / Cursor 2.0 — multi-agent UI with up to 8 parallel agents in isolated git worktrees.

  • Claude Code — CLI agent with CLAUDE.md for persistent context. Officially supports GitHub Actions since September 2025.

  • GitHub Copilot Workspace + coding agent — launched May 2025; >1M PRs authored in 5 months (Octoverse 2025).

L4 — From specifications to production

Full-stack build-from-prompt with DB, auth, deploy. You can go from "I want an app" to "it's deployed" without touching code directly.

Representative tools:

  • Bolt.new (StackBlitz) — in-browser generation with WebContainers.

  • Lovable — full-stack MVPs with auth and DB; went from USD 0 to USD 100M+ ARR in 8 months.

  • v0 (Vercel) — React/Next.js components from text or images.

  • Replit Agent 3 — autonomous for up to 200 minutes with its own tests.

  • Pythagora — all-in-one platform with specs, React frontend, Node backend.

L5 — Autonomous teams / agents

Multiple agents, long-term planning, independent decisions. The current frontier.

Main tools:

  • Devin (Cognition) — the only L5 agent with demonstrable massive enterprise adoption. Goldman Sachs, Citi, Dell, Cisco, Palantir, Nubank, Mercado Libre, Ramp use it in production.

  • GitHub Agent HQ (February 2026) — orchestrates Copilot, Claude Code, and OpenAI Codex as assignable agents from the same GitHub issue.

  • Cursor 2.0 multi-agent — up to 8 parallel agents in git worktrees.

  • MetaGPT — open source multi-agent coordination framework.

Real enterprise data (Devin, Jan 2026)

Cognition published Devin's metrics in its 2025 Performance Review:

  • 67% of PRs merged (vs. 34% in 2024).

  • 4x faster and 2x more efficient than the previous year.

  • 5–10% of total dev time saved on security remediation.

  • Test coverage rising from 50–60% to 80–90% in projects where it's applied.

  • Migrations at a 10–14x factor vs. human development (Oracle reports 14x faster on Java migrations).

⚠ Reality vs. Hype: although L5 sounds futuristic, 27% of organizations trust fully autonomous agents according to Capgemini 2025 (vs. 43% a year earlier). The trust curve inverts with maturity. Devin excels at junior tasks (migrations, tests, simple bugs) but requires human supervision for architecture and design decisions.

Alternative frameworks (in case you see them elsewhere)

  • Vellum (Dec 2025): L0 (Rule-Based) → L5 (Fully Creative).

  • AWS staged autonomy: 4 levels (Predefined → Dynamic → Partially → Fully autonomous). Most of prod at levels 2–3.

  • Knight First Amendment Institute (Jun 2025): taxonomy centered on the human's role (L1=Operator, L4=Approver, L5=Observer).

📖 Analysis of the L1–L5 framework: AI Coding Evolution and Landscape: L1 to L5

📖 Swarmia variant (recommended): Five levels of AI coding agent autonomy

Git with AI: how your concrete workflow changes

Of all the SDLC phases, there is one that is already completely permeated by AI in 2026 and that deserves its own section: Git, code review, and PRs. This applies every day, in every session of the master.

GitHub Copilot Code Review

Launched in preview in April 2025, progressive GA throughout 2025. The March 2026 data (GitHub Blog):

  • 60 million cumulative reviews.

  • 10x growth since launch.

  • >1 out of every 5 reviews on GitHub are already done by Copilot.

  • 71% of reviews leave actionable comments (29% correctly leave no comments — silence is better than noise).

  • Average: 5.1 comments per review.

  • 72.6% of users report improved effectiveness (Octoverse 2025).

📖 GitHub Blog: 60 million Copilot code reviews and counting

AI-generated commit messages

Standard flows in 2026:

  • Claude Code: built-in command (/commit or asking "generate a commit message based on the staged changes").

  • Cursor: "Generate commit message" button in the Git panel.

  • GitHub Copilot in VS Code: "Generate Commit Message" in the Source Control tab. Supports configurable styles (conventional commits, Gitmoji).

💡 Usage tip: AI doesn't know your business domain; a generated commit message might say "fix bug in user service" when the correct one would be "fix race condition in checkout when cart is empty". Always review before committing.

Resolving merge conflicts with AI

  • Cursor "Resolve in Chat": detects <<<<<<< / ======= / >>>>>>> and proposes a contextualized resolution.

  • Claude Code: claude resolve-conflicts or @claude resolve in GitHub Actions.

  • GitHub Copilot in VS Code: "Resolve in chat" button in the conflicts panel.

Copilot Workspace + coding agent

  • Launched in preview in April 2024, GA with coding agent in May 2025.

  • Assign an issue to Copilot → it creates a branch, implements, opens a PR.

  • In August 2025: 1.2M PRs generated/month.

  • Octoverse 2025: >1M PRs authored in 5 months (May–September 2025).

  • Agent HQ (February 2026): multi-vendor orchestration from GitHub.

Claude Code + GitHub Actions (official integration)

Launched on September 29, 2025 as part of Claude Code 2.0. Official repo: anthropics/claude-code-action.

Minimal setup:

# .github/workflows/claude.yml
name: Claude Code
on:
  issue_comment: { types: [created] }
  pull_request_review_comment: { types: [created] }

jobs:
  claude:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    permissions:
      contents: write
      pull-requests: write
      issues: write
      id-token: write
    steps:
      - uses: anthropics/claude-code-action@v1
        with:
          anthropic_api_key: ${{ secrets.ANTHROPIC_API_KEY }}
          # Default: Sonnet. Para Opus 4.7:
          # claude_args: --model claude-opus-4-7

Usage modes:

  • Interactive: mention @claude in PRs/issues; the agent responds and acts.

  • Automation: direct prompt in YAML; runs on every event.

Flexible auth: Anthropic API key, AWS Bedrock, GCP Vertex, Microsoft Foundry (with OIDC).

Prompt injection mitigation: filters HTML comments, invisible characters, image alt-text. Repo admins should configure allowed_bots and allowed_non_write_users carefully.

📖 Official documentation: Claude Code GitHub Actions

📖 Marketplace: Claude Code Action Official

Best practices for senior devs (2026)

  1. Treat the agent as a junior collaborator with a clear contract. Define CLAUDE.md / .cursor/rules / AGENTS.md (OpenAI's standard, already in >60,000 OSS repos) in each repo: code style, test commands, do/don't.

  2. Enforce human diff-review of any agentic change. 75% of senior devs (Stack Overflow 2025) manually review all AI code before merging.

  3. Strict CI gate. Block merge if tests/coverage/security checks don't pass. AI produces more code, but also more bugs (Faros AI 2026: incidents/PR +242%, bugs/dev +54% in teams with high adoption).

  4. Differentiate the PR's profile. Label auto-generated PRs (copilot, devin, claude-code). DORA 2025 suggests that adapting the process is what separates high-performance teams from those that struggle.

  5. Read-only MCP for code review. Connect the agent to the ticket tracker, staging database, and observability — but never with write permissions on prod without a human in the loop.

  6. Prefer async agents over chat for long tasks. Devin, Copilot coding agent, Cursor background agents leave a paper trail in Git, much more auditable than an ephemeral chat.

  7. Git hygiene matters more than ever. Atomic commits, conventional-format messages, short branches. Agents work better with a clean history.

Enterprise data: who is using this in production

So it doesn't sound like theory, the concrete names as of April 2026:

  • Goldman Sachs: first major bank to deploy Devin to its workforce of >12,000 developers. CIO Marco Argenti talks about a "hybrid workforce" and estimated 3–4x productivity.

  • Microsoft: 20–30% of internal code generated or suggested by AI (Satya Nadella, May 2025).

  • Anthropic: ~90% of code AI-assisted (Dario Amodei).

  • Salesforce: communications cite ">90% Cursor usage" among its 20,000 engineers [⚠ VERIFY: figure reported by third parties, no direct official confirmation].

  • Cognition customer list (January 2026): Goldman Sachs, Citi, Dell, Cisco, Ramp, Palantir, Nubank, Mercado Libre, OpenSea, Lumos, Microsoft, Curai Health, Santander.

This is not optional. It is the current state of the industry.

The actionable takeaways from this lesson

  1. The entire SDLC is being redefined, not just "writing code." Each phase has opportunities and risks. The master covers the ones with the greatest impact for a senior.

  2. L1–L5 is a map, not a quality hierarchy. Moving up a level isn't always better; each level serves different tasks. Knowing how to choose well is a senior skill.

  3. Code review went from "occasional task" to "core skill." When 1 out of every 5 reviews is already done by an AI, your role as a human reviewer is redefined: you filter what the AI can't see.

  4. Git with AI isn't a feature, it's the new default. Learn to generate commits, resolve conflicts, and configure GitHub Actions with Claude Code before S1.

  5. The big companies are already doing it. You're not arriving early; you're arriving on time.

Resources to go deeper

  • 📖 GitHub Octoverse 2025 — data on the state of the dev ecosystem.

Pre-course: Introduction to AI in Software Development — 102 min

🎥 Fundamentals: from Machine Learning to LLMs 🟢 — 19 min | AI4Devs 2026/06 Seniors

⏳ Estimated time: 19 min

The goal of this section is NOT for you to master the mathematical details of neural networks. It is for you to understand the bare minimum needed to not misuse your copilot. If you finish this lesson knowing what a token is, what the context window is, why LLMs hallucinate, and when to use RAG vs. fine-tuning, you've done your pre-course job.

Why a senior dev needs this

As a senior, you're not going to train models. You're going to prompt them, integrate them, supervise them, and debug their outputs. To do that well you need a correct mental model of what's underneath. Without it, you'll get frustrated for reasons that seem random but aren't:

  • "Why did the agent forget the file I gave it 5 messages ago?" → context window.

  • "Why did it invent an endpoint that doesn't exist?" → a mechanism of probability, not of truth.

  • "Why does the answer change every time even though the prompt is the same?" → temperature.

  • "Why doesn't it know what happened at my company last week?" → base model vs. RAG.

Those four misunderstandings cover 80% of the frustrations attributed to "AI is bad". In reality, they are misunderstandings about how it works.

Brief historical context (don't linger here)

Classic Machine Learning is divided into 4 categories that have existed for decades:

  • Supervised: learns from labeled data (spam/not spam, cat/dog photo). Algorithms: regression, KNN, SVM, trees.

  • Unsupervised: finds patterns without labels (clustering, recommendations). Algorithms: K-means, PCA.

  • Reinforcement: learns by trial and error with rewards (AlphaGo, robots). Algorithms: Q-learning, policy gradient.

  • Deep neural networks: layers of neurons that extract features automatically. Variants: CNN (images), RNN/LSTM (sequences), Transformer (text and everything else).

All of this is still alive and used in production. But the dominant paradigm today in developer tools is LLMs based on the Transformer + post-training with reinforcement. That's what you'll use every day in the master's program, so let's go straight there.

The Transformer revolution (2017)

📄 Original paper: Attention Is All You Need (Vaswani et al., NeurIPS 2017).

What changed: previous networks (RNN, LSTM) processed text sequentially — word by word, waiting for the previous one. The Transformer replaced recurrence with self-attention: each token can attend to all the others in parallel.

Why does this matter? Two reasons:

  1. Massive parallelization on GPUs: that's why scale exploded. RNNs couldn't be trained at 100B parameters because they were sequential. Transformers can.

  2. Long-distance context: a word at the end of the text can attend to a word at the beginning without "forgetting it". This solved the classic RNN problem with long sentences.

How self-attention works (the bare minimum)

For each token, the model computes three vectors: Query (Q), Key (K), and Value (V). The weight between tokens i and j is:

softmax(Q_i · K_j / √d_k)

In words: each token "asks" (Q) which other tokens are relevant to it, looks at the "keys" (K) of the others, and aggregates their "values" (V) weighted by relevance. This is done in parallel for the entire sequence.

Multi-head attention: different "heads" learn different relationships (syntactic, semantic, positional). That's why the same model understands both "the subject of the verb" and "the referent of the pronoun" at once.

💡 The intuition you take away: when you give context to your copilot (files, instructions, examples), the model is literally "paying attention" to parts of that context when generating each token. That's why context matters so much in your prompts.

📺 Essential interactive visualization:

https://poloclub.github.io/transformer-explainer/ (Polo Club / Georgia Tech, runs GPT-2 in your browser and lets you see the internal flow).

📺 3Blue1Brown — Visualizing Attention, a Transformer's Heart (the most intuitive visual explanation there is, in English with subtitles):

Model families

Although "LLM" sounds monolithic, there are three main families:

The commercial LLMs you use today are all decoder-only. When we talk about "LLMs" in the master's program we're almost always referring to this family.

Main LLM models (April 2026 snapshot)

💡 Don't memorize the figures. The table is so you have a map when you see names in tool documentation. What matters: the three leaders (GPT, Claude, Gemini) converged at ~1M tokens of context in 2026 — a qualitative change for developers.

Essential operational concepts

This is the section that pays off most to have clear before S1.

1. Tokens

What it is: the minimal unit the model processes. They are not letters or words, they are sub-words (BPE / SentencePiece).

Rules of thumb:

  • ~4 characters in English ≈ 1 token.

  • An average paragraph ≈ 100 tokens.

  • A typical README ≈ 2,000–4,000 tokens.

  • This entire pre-course ≈ 12,000–15,000 tokens.

Why it matters:

  • They cost money. APIs charge per token (input + output).

  • They define the context limit. The "context window" is measured in tokens.

  • Different models tokenize differently. Opus 4.7 introduced a new tokenizer that can use up to 35% more tokens on the same text in exchange for better performance.

🛠 Tool to experiment with: OpenAI Tokenizer — paste text and see how it gets segmented.

2. Context window

What it is: the maximum number of tokens (input + output) the model can consider at once. Everything inside that window is what the model "sees".

Current state (April 2026) — convergence toward ~1M tokens:

  • Claude Sonnet/Opus 4.6 and 4.7: 1M tokens standard.

  • GPT-5.5 API: 1,050,000 tokens (400K in Codex, 196K in ChatGPT Thinking).

  • Gemini 3 Pro: ~1M+ tokens.

  • Claude Haiku 4.5: 200K tokens.

What changed in the last 6 months: 1M tokens lets you load complete medium-sized repositories in a single prompt. This changes the architecture decision of "RAG vs. load the whole repo".

Watch out for "lost in the middle": even if the model has 1M of context, its retrieval capacity degrades in very long messages. Sonnet 4.6 improved substantially: 76% on 8-needle at 1M tokens vs. 18.5% for Sonnet 4.5.

3. Temperature, top-p, top-k

Parameters that control the randomness of the output:

  • Temperature (0 to 2): the higher, the more creative (and erratic). With temperature=0, the model is nearly deterministic.

  • Top-p (nucleus sampling): truncates to the cumulative probability mass. Top-p=0.95 considers only the tokens that add up to 95% probability.

  • Top-k: considers only the k most probable tokens.

For brainstorming, creative writing, naming: temperature 0.7–1.0.

💡 Tip: when an agent generates different code each time with the same prompt, the cause is usually temperature > 0. In the master's program we work with low temperatures so the demos are reproducible.

4. Embeddings

What they are: dense vectors (typically 1024–4096 dimensions) that encode meaning. Two texts with similar meaning have embeddings close to each other in vector space.

What they're used for:

  • Semantic search: "find documents about this topic, even if they don't use the same words".

  • RAG (Retrieval-Augmented Generation): the technical foundation for an LLM to answer questions about your private documents.

  • MCP: many MCP servers use embeddings for retrieval (e.g. an internal docs server).

Leading models 2026:

  • OpenAI text-embedding-3-large

  • Voyage 3

  • Cohere Embed v3

  • BGE (open source)

💡 Why it matters for you: when you configure MCP against your internal knowledge base, you'll be using embeddings without knowing it. Knowing what they are helps you debug when retrieval returns weird results.

5. Why LLMs hallucinate

This is the most common confusion among devs unfamiliar with the topic.

How generation works: an LLM generates token by token, choosing at each step the most probable one given the context. It does not validate against reality. It doesn't "know" that an endpoint exists — it knows that, given the pattern "API endpoint for users", the most plausible continuation is /api/users.

When the context is ambiguous or the data isn't in the training, the model continues with the most plausible continuation. That's what we call "hallucinating". It's not a bug — it's exactly how the architecture works.

Andrej Karpathy describes it as:

  • "Jagged intelligence": brilliant in some areas, surprisingly bad in others.

  • "Anterograde amnesia": it doesn't consolidate knowledge between sessions; it only has short-term memory in the context window.

Recent improvements:

  • GPT-5 reduced errors ~45% vs. GPT-4o.

  • Sonnet 4.6 improved substantially in long-context retrieval.

  • Reasoning models ("thinking" in Claude, "o1/o3" in OpenAI) reduce hallucinations in tasks with structured reasoning.

What to do about it:

  • Give it explicit context (files, docs, schemas).

  • Ask it to cite the source when applicable.

  • Verify critical outputs (packages, endpoints, external APIs — those are the points where it hallucinates most).

6. Base model vs. instruction-tuned vs. RLHF vs. RLVR

A progression of how a modern LLM is trained:

  1. Base: pre-training on a raw corpus (the whole internet). It only predicts the next token. It does not follow instructions, it's not useful as a chatbot.

  2. Instruction-tuned: supervised fine-tuning on (instruction, response) pairs. It learns to follow instructions.

  3. RLHF (Reinforcement Learning from Human Feedback): humans rank responses, the model learns to generate the preferred ones. What made ChatGPT "kind and helpful".

  4. RLVR (Reinforcement Learning from Verifiable Rewards): emerged in 2025. Trained on math/code/logic tasks with a verifiable reward (does the code pass the tests?). This gave birth to reasoning models (DeepSeek R1, OpenAI o1/o3, Claude "thinking", Sonnet 4.6 adaptive thinking).

💡 Why it matters: when you see "Opus thinking" or "GPT-5 thinking" in a tool, you're activating a model trained with RLVR that thinks before answering. It costs more latency but reduces errors on complex tasks. Models without "thinking" are faster and cheaper for simple tasks.

7. Fine-tuning vs. RAG (the most common decision you'll face)

Practical rule:

  • Does your data change frequently? → RAG.

  • Do you need a very specific style or format that the base model doesn't get right? → fine-tuning.

  • In practice, 90% of a senior dev's cases are solved with RAG (via MCP), not with fine-tuning.

💡 MCP is the standard RAG pattern in 2026. When you configure an MCP server against your knowledge base, you're doing RAG without writing retrieval code.

What's new in LLMs in the last 6 months (what a dev should know)

  1. 1M context without a price premium in Claude Opus/Sonnet 4.6, Gemini 3, and GPT-5.5 — rethinks the "RAG vs. load the entire repo" decision.

  2. Reasoning as a toggle (Sonnet 4.6 "adaptive thinking", GPT-5 "thinking budget") — start thinking about cost/latency vs. depth of reasoning explicitly.

  3. Persistent client-side memory (Cursor memories, Claude memory, ChatGPT memory) — changes the day-to-day prompting pattern.

  4. Significant reduction in hallucinations in SOTA: GPT-5 ~45% fewer errors than GPT-4o, Sonnet 4.6 long-context retrieval 76% (vs. 18.5%).

  5. New tokenizers (Opus 4.7) that use more tokens but perform better — the cost in USD per task doesn't always go down with each new version.

The actionable takeaways from this lesson

  1. Tokens, context window, temperature, embeddings — these are the 4 concepts you'll use every day. Master them before S1.

  2. LLMs don't "know", they predict probabilistically. When they hallucinate, it's not a bug — it's the architecture. Your job as a senior is to give them the context they need and verify what's critical.

  3. RAG > fine-tuning for almost all your cases as a dev. And in 2026, RAG = MCP.

  4. "Thinking" is not free. Use it when the task requires it (complex debugging, multi-step planning), not for trivial tasks.

  5. Don't memorize model versions. They change every 2-3 months. Memorize the concepts — those have been stable since 2017.

Resources to go deeper

The essentials (in this order)

  • 📺 Transformer Explainer (Polo Club / Georgia Tech) — interactive tool, runs GPT-2 in your browser.

  • 📺 3Blue1Brown — Visualizing Attention, a Transformer's Heart — the most intuitive visual explanation.

  • 📖 Jay Alammar — The Illustrated Transformer — the canonical classic.

In Spanish

  • 📺 dotCSV — series on reasoning models and LLMs 2024–2025 — search the channel.

  • 📺 SobernIA — Descifrando los Secretos de los Transformers.

If you want to go really deep

  • 📺 Andrej Karpathy — Neural Networks: Zero to Hero — the best technical course for devs. You build GPT from scratch.

  • 📺 Andrej Karpathy — Let's build GPT from scratch — 2h, intensive.

  • 📖 Karpathy — 2025 LLM Year in Review — what changed in 2025, written by Karpathy.
